J	Auriol, E; Lassebie, J; Panin, A; Raiber, E; Seabright, P				Auriol, Emmanuelle; Lassebie, Julie; Panin, Amma; Raiber, Eva; Seabright, Paul			GOD INSURES THOSE WHO PAY? FORMAL INSURANCE AND RELIGIOUS OFFERINGS IN GHANA	QUARTERLY JOURNAL OF ECONOMICS			English	Article							PENTECOSTALISM; REPLICABILITY; SACRIFICE; SALIENCE; BELIEF; INCOME; GOODS; VIEW	This article provides experimental support for the hypothesis that insurance can be a motive for religious donations. We randomize enrollment of members of a Pentecostal church in Ghana into a commercial funeral insurance policy. Then church members allocate money between themselves and a set of religious goods in a series of dictator games with significant stakes. Members enrolled in insurance give significantly less money to their own church compared with members who only receive information about the insurance. Enrollment also reduces giving toward other spiritual goods. We set up a model exploring different, channels of religiously based insurance. The implications of the model and the results from the dictator games suggest that adherents perceive the church as a source of insurance and that this insurance is derived from beliefs in an interventionist God. Survey results suggest that material insurance from the church community is also important and we hypothesize that these two insurance channels exist in parallel.	[Auriol, Emmanuelle; Seabright, Paul] Toulouse Sch Econ, Toulouse, France; [Auriol, Emmanuelle; Seabright, Paul] Inst Adv Study Toulouse, Toulouse, France; [Auriol, Emmanuelle] Org Econ Cooperat & Dev, Louvain, Belgium; [Lassebie, Julie; Seabright, Paul] Catholic Univ Louvain, Louvain Inst Data Anal & Modeling, Ctr Operat Res & Econometr, Louvain, Belgium; [Panin, Amma; Raiber, Eva] Aix Marseille Univ, Ctr Natl Rech Sci, Ecole Hautes Etud Sci Sociales, Cent Marseille,Aix Marseille Sch Econ, Marseille, France	Universite de Toulouse; Universite Toulouse 1 Capitole; Toulouse School of Economics; Organisation for Economic Co-operation & Development (OECD); Universite Catholique Louvain; Centre National de la Recherche Scientifique (CNRS); UDICE-French Research Universities; Aix-Marseille Universite	Auriol, E (corresponding author), Toulouse Sch Econ, Toulouse, France. J	Caplin, A; Csaba, D; Leahy, J; Nov, O				Caplin, Andrew; Csaba, Daniel; Leahy, John; Nov, Oded			RATIONAL INATTENTION, COMPETITIVE SUPPLY, AND PSYCHOMETRICS	QUARTERLY JOURNAL OF ECONOMICS			English	Article							INFORMATION; CHOICE; PRODUCTIVITY; FRICTIONS; SALIENCE; ATTENTION; POLLUTION	We introduce a simple method of recovering attention costs from choice data. Our method rests on a precise analogy with production theory. Costs of attention determine consumer demand and consumer welfare, just as a competitive firm's technology determines its supply curve and profits. We implement our recovery method experimentally, outline applications, and link our work to the broader literature on inattention and mistaken decisions. J	Bazzi, S; Koehler-Derrick, G; Marx, B				Bazzi, Samuel; Koehler-Derrick, Gabriel; Marx, Benjamin			THE INSTITUTIONAL FOUNDATIONS OF RELIGIOUS POLITICS: EVIDENCE FROM INDONESIA	QUARTERLY JOURNAL OF ECONOMICS			English	Article							ECONOMIC-PERFORMANCE; ISLAMIC LAW; WAQF; INEQUALITY; EDUCATION; ORIGINS; SOCIETY; CULTURE; IMPACT; GOODS	This article explores the foundations of religious influence in politics and society. We show that an important Islamic institution fostered the entrenchment of Islamism at a critical juncture in Indonesia, the world's largest Muslim country. In the early 1960s, rural elites transferred large amounts of land into waqf-inalienable charitable trusts in Islamic law-to avoid expropriation by the state. Regions facing a greater threat of expropriation exhibit more prevalent waqf land and Islamic institutions endowed as such, including mosques and religious schools. These endowments provided conservative forces with the capital needed to promote Islamist ideology and mobilize against the secular state. We identify lasting effects of the transfers on the size of the religious sector, electoral support for Islamist parties, and the adoption of local sharia laws. These effects are shaped by greater demand for religion in government but not by greater piety among the electorate. Waqf assets also impose costs on the local economy, particularly in agriculture, where these endowments are associated with lower productivity. Overall, our findings shed new light on the origins and consequences of Islamism.
